This means the rule is no longer in effect.\n\n## Previous Rule Content (Before Abrogation)\n\nBefore being abrogated, the debtor in a chapter 13 case could file a modified plan anytime before confirmation, and the plan had to conform to Local Form 3015-1 except that it was labeled \"Modified Plan,\" with notice given to the trustee, the United States Trustee, and creditors whose treatment was adversely changed.\n\nThe notice had to be given not later than seven days or mailed not later than ten days before the confirmation hearing, and objections to a modified plan filed preconfirmation had to be filed and served not later than 24 hours prior to the confirmation hearing.\n\n## Current Procedures\n\nSince Rule 3015-2 has been abrogated, plan modification procedures are now governed by Rule 3015-1, which covers Chapter 12 or 13 – Filing Plans, Modifying Plans, and Objecting to Confirmation.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"e141b911-bd06-43a8-85b4-f06139486c12","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:25:18.172Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0dc9244c-a2d8-4c97-ae7e-0fcfdaa89f96","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

The plan as modified becomes the plan unless, after notice and a hearing, such modification is disapproved.\n\n## Time Limitations\n\nA plan modified under this section may not provide for payments over a period that expires after three years after the time that the first payment under the original confirmed plan was due, unless the court, for cause, approves a longer period, but the court may not approve a period that expires after five years after such time.\n\n## Lack of Substantive Standards\n\nOne key aspect of § 1329 is that although Congress wrote into the section several procedural requirements and limitations on what may and may not be done by modification of a plan, the section contains no substantive standard or test for a court to use in determining whether to grant or deny a request for plan modification, making it a rare example of Congress granting to bankruptcy judges almost unfettered discretion in ruling on motions made under that section.\n\nThe search results indicate no significant changes to § 1329 between 2024 and 2025, as the statute and its interpretation remain consistent with prior case law.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b869d8d8-52ee-498b-a2ba-5257dc175099","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:25:19.938Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"445bfbbf-4c93-466b-9b7d-bb20646c581d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Bankruptcy § 505 | FindLaw\",\"url\":\"https://codes.findlaw.com/us/title-11-bankruptcy/11-usc-sect-505/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's information about 11 USC 505(a) regarding tax liability determination in bankruptcy court procedures:\n\n## Overview of 11 USC 505(a)\n\nUnder 11 USC 505(a)(1), the court may determine the amount or legality of any tax, any fine or penalty relating to a tax, or any addition to tax, whether or not previously assessed, whether or not paid, and whether or not contested before and adjudicated by a judicial or administrative tribunal of competent jurisdiction.\n\n## Scope of Bankruptcy Court Authority\n\nThe legislative statements accompanying § 505 make clear that the section authorizes the bankruptcy court to rule on the merits of any tax claim involving an unpaid tax, fine, or penalty relating to a tax, or any addition to a tax, of the debtor or the estate. Section 505(a)(1) vests the bankruptcy court with broad jurisdiction over tax matters of the estate and the debtor, including determinations with respect to the personal liability of the debtor.\n\n## Limitations\n\nThe bankruptcy court will not have jurisdiction to rule on the merits of any tax claim which has been previously adjudicated, in a contested proceeding, before a court of competent jurisdiction. A proceeding in the U.S. Tax Court is to be considered \"contested\" if the debtor filed a petition in the Tax Court by the commencement of the case and the Internal Revenue Service had filed an answer to the petition.\n\n## Court Authority After Determination\n\nAfter determination by the court of a tax under this section, the governmental unit charged with responsibility for collection of such tax may assess such tax against the estate, the debtor, or a successor to the debtor, as the case may be, subject to any otherwise applicable law.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"51428422-2ee9-48e3-a42a-89e13fb94d1c","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:25:20.256Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"87cdd853-cee6-44a3-a4c8-d7db9dabf03a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Here's what the search found:\n\n## FRBP 1016 - Death or Incompetency of a Debtor\n\nIf a reorganization, family farmer's debt adjustment, or individual's debt adjustment case is pending under chapter 11, chapter 12, or chapter 13, the case may be dismissed; or if further administration is possible and in the best interest of the parties, the case may proceed and be concluded in the same manner, so far as possible, as though the death or incompetency had not occurred.\n\n## Key Case Law\n\nThe search revealed several relevant cases:\n\n- Most courts hold that in the Chapter 13 proceeding, if the debtor dies or becomes incapacitated after the Chapter 13 plan is confirmed, the case will not be dismissed but will proceed to its conclusion.\n\n- To avoid having a reorganization case dismissed upon the death of the debtor, an interested party must show that further administration of the case is in the best interest of the parties. The decision to proceed with the reorganization is entirely within the bankruptcy court's discretion.\n\n- The Rule does not preclude further administration where the Chapter 13 plan has not been confirmed prior to death, and it is possible under certain circumstances for the court to confirm a plan after the death of the debtor.\n\nThe search results cite cases including *In re Querner*, 7 F.3d 1199 (5th Cir. 1993); *In re Perkins*, 381 B.R. 530 (Bankr. Here's a summary of the key findings:\n\n## Mortgage Servicer Misconduct in Chapter 13 Bankruptcy\n\n**Discharge Violations and Overstating Arrears**\n\nBanks violate the bankruptcy discharge if they don't credit Chapter 13 plan payments to a home mortgage properly, which exposes them to sanctions, including payment of the debtor's attorneys fees and actual damages. A notable example is Scott v. Caliber Home Loans, Inc., where Caliber agreed the homeowner was current on the mortgage at the end of Chapter 13, but then announced $1,700 in unpaid fees and threatened foreclosure, leading the bankruptcy judge to find Caliber willfully violated the discharge.\n\n**Rule 3002.1 and Disclosure Requirements**\n\nA Chapter 13-specific rule adopted in 2012, Rule 3002.1 requires a mortgage creditor to give notices of payment changes and fees added to the loan during Chapter 13 and to file an accounting if it contends the homeowner isn't current at the end of the case.\n\n**Automatic Stay Violations and Sanctions**\n\nIf a creditor knowingly violates the automatic stay, they can be held in contempt of court, especially when the creditor has received proper notice of the bankruptcy filing but chooses to pursue collection efforts anyway, with courts imposing sanctions to deter future violations. If the court finds that the servicer violated the discharge injunction, a wider array of sanctions are available.\n\n**Remedies for Debtors**\n\nDebtors who suffer harm from an automatic stay violation can be awarded compensatory damages, including actual damages that may cover lost wages, repossession costs, returned check fees, or other out-of-pocket losses.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"27d6aae5-1d3e-408d-b093-a428cfd4087d","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:25:35.697Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"09eda65b-fb2b-4b9d-a453-551893b5187c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## 401(k) Protection and Exemption Status\n\n401k funds are generally exempt assets under federal bankruptcy law, protecting them from creditors and preserving retirement security. However, funds withdrawn from a 401k lose their exempt status, becoming part of the bankruptcy estate and potentially accessible to creditors.\n\n## Court Approval Requirements\n\nIf you want to take out a 401k loan during Chapter 13 bankruptcy, you must obtain court permission first because the loan payment will be a new expense. You'll need to file a motion explaining the particular circumstances of your case. The bankruptcy judge will review the facts, including whether it's feasible to pay your nonpriority unsecured debts less—the creditors who fall lowest in payment priority—before approving or denying the loan request.\n\n## Section 1306 - Property of the Estate\n\nSection 1306 broadens the definition of property of the estate for chapter 13 purposes to include all property acquired and all earnings from services performed by the debtor after the commencement of the case.\n\n## Court Discretion\n\nWhether you're allowed to withdraw from your 401(k) to pay off a Chapter 13 depends on the court. The court stated in dictum that voluntary post-petition contributions to a 401(k) plan may never be deducted from calculation of disposable income.\n\n**In re Davis:**\nThe Sixth Circuit held that a chapter 13 debtor's post-petition voluntary contributions to an employer-sponsored retirement plan are not part of her disposable income so long as they are a continuation of pre-petition contributions. On June 1, 2020 the Sixth Circuit reversed the bankruptcy judge, ruling that the 2005 change in bankruptcy laws excluded retirement plan contributions from disposable income, which allowed Ms. Davis (and all Chapter 13 debtors) to continue making retirement contributions while they were in Chapter 13.\n\nIn essence, the Davis court rejected its own dictum in Seafort, establishing that debtors can continue pre-petition 401(k) contributions during Chapter 13 bankruptcy without those contributions being counted toward disposable income.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5103eedd-08b5-4567-aec0-eee2243d798f","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:25:58.613Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"39ab6d13-7699-4dc3-941d-405776651bb9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Overview of 12 USC 2605 and Escrow Requirements\n\nIf the terms of any federally related mortgage loan require the borrower to make payments to the servicer of the loan for deposit into an escrow account for the purpose of assuring payment of taxes, insurance premiums, and other charges with respect to the property, the servicer shall make payments from the escrow account for such taxes, insurance premiums, and other charges in a timely manner as such payments become due.\n\n## Bankruptcy-Specific Issues\n\nA particularly relevant case involved mortgage servicers' handling of escrow accounts during bankruptcy. Defendant failed to timely analyze escrow accounts for borrowers who filed for bankruptcy. This was cited as a RESPA violation in enforcement actions brought against mortgage servicers.\n\n## Statutory Damages\n\nPlaintiffs may obtain actual damages for the servicer's failure to comply with the law, and they may also claim statutory damages not to exceed $2,000 per individual if a defendant has \"a pattern or practice of noncompliance with the requirements of this section.\"\n\n## Pattern or Practice Standard\n\nDistrict courts have interpreted the phrase \"a pattern or practice of noncompliance with this section\" to require the plaintiffs to allege that the defendant routinely violated any RESPA section to plausibly state a claim for statutory damages pursuant to § 2605(g).\n\nThe search results show that servicers' failures to properly analyze and manage escrow accounts for borrowers in bankruptcy have been pursued as RESPA violations under 12 USC 2605.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"ecd2d2fb-9a46-42c0-ac22-629dadcc6e0d","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:02.018Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b38d45f8-e5cf-410b-974d-61d84d47425e","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Here's what the law provides:\n\n## The 120-Day Rule\n\nThe claim must either be denied or not acted upon for 120 days (rather than the six months provided in IRC 6532), according to 11 USC 505(a)(2)(B).\n\nMore specifically, bankruptcy courts cannot determine \"any right of the estate to a tax refund, before the earlier of—(i) 120 days after the trustee properly requests such refund from the governmental unit from which such refund is claimed; or (ii) a determination by such governmental unit of such request\".\n\n## Purpose and Jurisdiction\n\nBecause of the bankruptcy aim to close the estate as expeditiously as possible, the House amendment shortens to 120 days the period for the Internal Revenue Service to decide the refund claim. This contrasts with the standard non-bankruptcy rule where a suit for refund of Federal taxes cannot be filed until 6 months after a claim for refund is filed with the Internal Revenue Service.\n\n## Bankruptcy Court Authority After 120 Days\n\nIf the Internal Revenue Service, or other tax authority does not rule on the refund claim within 120 days, then the bankruptcy court may rule on the merits of the refund claim.\n\n## Procedural Requirements\n\nBankruptcy courts are statutorily prohibited from determining tax refunds unless the two procedural requirements in § 505(a)(2)(B) are met: (1) the trustee must \"properly request\" the tax refund from the governmental authority; and (2) the governmental authority must rule on the trustee's refund request or 120 must elapse.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"fe411f2f-bddb-4031-8881-13f441ae4925","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:02.423Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7c7a1117-4c0b-4056-8035-5eb3bd406210","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

The rule applies in a chapter 13 case to claims that are (1) secured by a security interest in the debtor's principal residence, and (2) provided for under §1322(b)(5) of the Code in the debtor's plan.\n\n## Notice Requirements for Payment Changes\n\nThe holder of the claim shall file and serve on the debtor, debtor's counsel, and the trustee a notice of any change in the payment amount, including any change that results from an interest rate or escrow account adjustment, no later than 21 days before a payment in the new amount is due.\n\n## Notice of Fees, Expenses, and Charges\n\nAn itemized notice must be given, within 180 days of incurrence, of any postpetition fees, expenses, or charges that the holder of the claim asserts are recoverable from the debtor or against the debtor's principal residence. This might include, for example, inspection fees, late charges, or attorney's fees.\n\n## Purpose of Notices\n\nTimely notice of these changes will permit the debtor or trustee to challenge the validity of any such charges, if appropriate, and to adjust postpetition mortgage payments.\n\n## Cure Determination\n\nWithin 45 days after the debtor completes all payments due to the trustee under a Chapter 13 plan, the trustee must file a notice stating what amount the trustee disbursed to the claim holder to cure any default and whether it has been cured.\n\n## Sanctions for Non-Compliance\n\nIf the claim holder fails to provide any information as required by this rule, the court may, after notice and a hearing, do one or more of the following: (1) preclude the holder from presenting the omitted information in any form as evidence in a contested matter or adversary proceeding in the case—unless the court determines that the failure was substantially justified or is harmless; (2) award other appropriate relief, including reasonable expenses and attorney's fees caused by the failure.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"df9d8ad6-faaa-471f-a858-c150ac9b53e8","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:16.655Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5ba5a082-c7ab-486f-ac64-1b152d62ab8f","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

This exclusion applies to Social Security survivor benefits as well.\n\n### Application to Chapter 13 Bankruptcy\n\nIn Chapter 13 cases, Social Security benefits must be reported in the income filing but are not included in the calculation of disposable income payable. Social Security funds won't be included in the calculations on the means test forms used to determine how much disposable income a debtor must contribute to their repayment plan.\n\n### Court Precedent\n\nThe Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that a Chapter 13 debtor's Social Security income is not included in calculating the debtor's projected disposable income. The Court reasoned that since Congress excluded Social Security income from \"current monthly income\" in Code § 101(10A) and \"disposable income\" in Code § 1325(b)(2), it made little sense to circumvent that prohibition by allowing Social Security income to be included in projected disposable income.\n\nThis protection applies to all Social Security benefits, including survivor benefits, ensuring they are protected during Chapter 13 bankruptcy proceedings.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0187201b-1604-41e6-a9f4-7969685c9977","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:28.596Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cae87956-87ab-4c3c-9ebd-0a073b5a86d8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Basics\n\nThe equation is: Current Monthly Income minus Allowed Expenses equals Disposable Income. In Chapter 13, the payment is driven by disposable income, but disposable income is defined by the Bankruptcy Code — not just your real-world spending.\n\n## IRS Standards vs. Actual Expenses\n\nThe Internal Revenue Service (IRS) issues National and Local Standards for certain expense amounts, which are used to answer questions in the disposable income calculation. Debtors must deduct the expense amounts set out in the initial calculation regardless of actual expense, but in later parts of the form, some actual expenses may be used if they are higher than the standards.\n\nStandardized costs for deductions for food and utilities are based on IRS National and Local Standards, while actual costs are used for mortgages and taxes.\n\n## Post-Confirmation Modifications and Actual Expenses\n\nFor secured debts like mortgages, the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit ruled that above-median income earners who filed for Chapter 13 bankruptcy may use their actual mortgage payments when calculating their disposable income. This represents a significant development regarding the use of actual expenses versus standards in disposable income calculations.\n\nThe search results primarily address initial plan calculations on Official Form 122C-2 rather than specific guidance on Section 1329 modifications post-confirmation. Complaints can be filed by mail to the OLPR or online through their website.\n\n## Investigation Timeline\n\nMost DEC investigations take four to seven months, and in-house investigations can take longer, with the goal to address dismissals or private discipline within one year. This can take more than a year to get to public discipline due to the volume of complaints received.\n\n## Organizational Structure\n\nThe RLPR create three entities to help the Court hold lawyers accountable for violations of the MRPC: the Director of the Office of Lawyers Professional Responsibility (Director or OLPR), the Lawyers Professional Responsibility Board (LPRB or Board), and the District Ethics Committees (DECs or committees).\n\n## Investigation Process\n\nEach DEC investigates complaints of misconduct against lawyers located in that district that have been referred to the DEC by the Director and makes recommendations to the Director. A majority of complaints are investigated but there are some types of complaints that are often dismissed without investigation, including routine fee disputes, most matters pending in court (unless the misconduct is clear or the judge has referred the matter to the OLPR), most complaints against court-appointed counsel in criminal or civil matters while the matter is ongoing, most complaints against prosecutors who have discretion regarding the crimes they may charge, and complaints regarding minor personal conduct of lawyers outside of the practice of law.\n\n## Discipline Options\n\nThe discipline options include private admonitions, private probation, and several public discipline options including public reprimands, suspension and disbarment. Private discipline is reserved for misconduct that is isolated and nonserious or minor misconduct across a couple of matters that makes probation a helpful tool for the lawyer and their clients. Public discipline is for all other conduct that cannot be considered isolated and nonserious (serious misconduct).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"743c1fe3-60bd-4222-86eb-efbdfd9c6322","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:32.019Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"8d8d383a-6bf6-4861-a909-66810b28fc01","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

If the Internal Revenue Service does not rule on the refund claim within 120 days, then the bankruptcy court may rule on the merits of the refund claim.\n\n## Turnover of Property (Section 542)\n\nIf the debtor had the right to claim a tax refund, the right to claim a refund and to bring a refund suit became property of the estate, and such a refund claim would be a debt owed to the debtor and should be paid to a Chapter 7 trustee under section 542(b), with the Service having administrative procedures for Chapter 7 trustees to request refunds that are property of the estate.\n\nSection 542(a) requires that the property to be turned over to the Trustee be in \"possession, custody, or control\" of the turnover target.\n\n## Determination Authority\n\nThe bankruptcy court can determine the right of the estate to a tax refund if the taxing authority does not rule on the trustee's refund claim within 120 calendar days under 11 USC 505(a)(2)(B).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"bf8fe540-4606-4ae6-bdb5-dcd9e4159b24","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:52.591Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d649b6cc-d91d-4ace-98f2-cfaa7bc6f616","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

The IRS may offset refunds from postpetition periods against postpetition tax debts (and may offset refunds from prepetition periods against prepetition tax debts).\n\n## Contacting the IRS About Refund Status\n\nIf you believe your refund has been held, delayed, turned over, or offset against your tax debts, you can check on its status by contacting the IRS Centralized Insolvency Operation at 800-973-0424 Monday through Friday, 7 a.m. to 8 p.m. Eastern time.\n\n## Processing Procedures\n\nIf you need to contact a specialist handling your case regarding bankruptcy matters, you can call 800-973-0424 to get their name and phone number, and the specialist will ordinarily amend the proof of claim within 21 days. D. Mont. 2010), is a significant bankruptcy case that addresses 401(k) contributions during Chapter 13.\n\n## The Prigge Interpretation\n\nUnder the Prigge interpretation, debtors are never permitted to contribute to voluntary retirement plans during Chapter 13. This represents one approach taken by some bankruptcy courts, though it's important to note that courts don't uniformly follow this strict interpretation.\n\n## General 401(k) Withdrawal Rules in Chapter 13\n\nWhile the Prigge case concerns contributions during bankruptcy, the broader legal framework regarding 401(k) withdrawals in Chapter 13 includes:\n\n- Your 401(k) has unlimited bankruptcy protection, but once withdrawn, the funds lose this protection, which means you'll likely lose the money to creditors in bankruptcy.\n\n- Withdrawing from a 401(k) in a Chapter 13 would have to be approved by the court because the debtor must commit all of her disposable monthly income to the Chapter 13 plan. Additionally, a 401(k) withdrawal in a Chapter 13 cannot be used to make the monthly plan payments.\n\nThe case illustrates the tension between protecting retirement savings and ensuring creditors receive appropriate payments from debtors' disposable income.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b79ec4a7-2d15-4d6e-90a3-c2825476ffec","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:26:58.370Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b0761870-51b3-4829-8124-79e71e155fd2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Lanning and the forward-looking approach to projected disposable income in Chapter 13 bankruptcy:\n\n## Case Overview\n\nHamilton v. Lanning was decided on June 7, 2010, and addressed how debtors filing for protection under Chapter 13 of the Bankruptcy Code must agree to a court-approved plan under which they pay creditors out of their future income.\n\n## The Central Issue\n\nAlthough the court found both parties' interpretations of \"projected disposable income\" problematic, it ruled that Lanning's \"forward-looking\" approach based on future income was preferable to Hamilton's \"mechanical\" approach.\n\n## The Forward-Looking Approach\n\nThe Supreme Court held that a bankruptcy court, when determining a debtor's \"disposable income\" for purposes of a bankruptcy plan, may properly consider evidence that the debtor's income or expenses have changed or are virtually certain to change in the future, compared to the amounts during the six months immediately before the bankruptcy petition was filed.\n\nThe term \"projected\" is used in many federal statutes in contexts that do not connote simple mathematical calculation from past data, and courts before the enactment of the Bankruptcy Abuse Prevention and Consumer Protection Act of 2005 generally recognized that a calculation based on past income was only the first step in determining projected disposable income and that they had discretion to account for changes in the debtor's circumstances.\n\n## Key Distinction\n\n\"Projected disposable income\" is a \"forward-looking\" term and is not equivalent to \"disposable income.\"\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3c7f7faa-5b6d-44c7-a5dd-0218979e3709","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:27:11.681Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a475e8f7-a91a-44bf-9258-926e7eb8cf12","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Here's what the research shows:\n\n## Statutory Framework and Damages\n\nAn individual injured by any willful violation of a stay provided by Section 362 shall recover actual damages, including costs and attorneys' fees, and, in appropriate circumstances, may recover punitive damages.\n\n## Three-Part Test for § 362(k) Claims\n\nA debtor seeking damages under this provision bears the burden of proving, by a preponderance of the evidence, three elements: \"(1) that a violation of the automatic stay occurred; (2) that the violation was willfully committed; and (3) that the debtor suffered damages as a result of the violation.\"\n\n## Willfulness Standard\n\nA willful violation does not require a specific intent to violate the automatic stay. Willful violation does not require a specific intent to violate the automatic stay. Rather, the statute provides for damages upon a finding that the creditor knew of the automatic stay and that the defendant's actions which violated the stay were taken.\n\n## Attorney Fees\n\nA creditor is liable only for debtor's \"attorney fees related to enforcing the automatic stay and remedying the stay violation.\" An award of fees reasonably incurred is mandated by the statute; courts awarding fees under § 362(k) thus retain the discretion to eliminate unnecessary or plainly excessive fees. The reasonableness of fees is determined in accordance with § 330 of the Bankruptcy Code.\n\n## Punitive Damages Standard\n\nEgregious, intentional misconduct on the violator's part is necessary to support a punitive damages award.\n\n## Notable Mortgage Servicer Cases\n\nA bankruptcy court in another state recently awarded punitive damages of $500,000 as necessary to deter a mortgage servicer from committing further stay violations after evidence revealed the existence of unwritten and undisclosed policies, and express procedures adopted to narrow the sources of bankruptcy information that the servicer was willing to acknowledge.\n\nThe Third Circuit ruled that an action under Section 362(k) to recover damages for a willful violation of the automatic stay may exist independent of the underlying bankruptcy case. The debtor's failure to complete plan payments must be due to circumstances beyond the debtor's control and through no fault of the debtor.\n\n2. **Adequate Creditor Payment**: Creditors must have received at least as much as they would have received in a chapter 7 liquidation case.\n\n3. **Plan Modification Not Possible**: Modification of the plan must not be possible.\n\n### Death of Spouse as Qualifying Hardship\n\nA debtor may need to obtain an early discharge due to a disability, death, or divorce making it difficult to complete even a modified plan. More specifically, if the Chapter 13 is a joint case and the spouse dies, the surviving spouse could ask the court to lower the plan payments if the loss of income is significant, or could ask the court for a hardship discharge to receive the discharge early.\n\nDeath is one of the most common precursors to a Chapter 13 bankruptcy hardship discharge.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6f706c52-dfa4-4fc4-ba03-1ef1bfcd3e98","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:27:33.998Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"34e94654-8352-4fee-a660-3254d699a1f1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

One of the advantages to a cure and maintain plan is that it does not depend on the lender's approval.\n\n**Plan Modification for Post-Petition Arrears**\n\nA debtor may propose to pay post-petition arrears through a modified plan. Here are the key points:\n\n## Rule 1.3 - Diligence\n\nA lawyer shall act with reasonable diligence and promptness in representing a client. A lawyer should pursue a matter on behalf of a client despite opposition, obstruction, or personal inconvenience to the lawyer, and take whatever lawful and ethical measures are required to vindicate a client's cause or endeavor, and must also act with commitment and dedication to the interests of the client and with zeal in advocacy upon the client's behalf. The lawyer's duty to act with reasonable diligence does not require the use of offensive tactics or preclude the treating of all persons involved in the legal process with courtesy and respect.\n\n## Rule 1.4 - Communication\n\nA lawyer shall explain a matter to the extent reasonably necessary to permit the client to make informed decisions regarding the representation. The lawyer must keep the client reasonably informed about the status of the matter, such as significant developments affecting the timing or the substance of the representation.\n\n## Bankruptcy Context\n\nRegarding attorney duties in bankruptcy matters, the search results indicate that a lawyer who knows that the lawyer's conduct could reasonably be the basis for a non-frivolous malpractice claim by a current client that materially affects the client's interests has duties to act under the Minnesota Rules of Professional Conduct, with particular attention to Rules 1.4 and 1.7.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6012a3d4-5019-4a21-b010-1d39062d8599","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:27:49.483Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6a73c014-2fd4-4243-b3ac-f431eca40816","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

SCS Credit Corp. Overview:**\nThe \"Till rate\" is named after the U.S. Supreme Court case, Till v. SCS Credit Corp., 541 U.S. 465 (2004), which established the standard for determining interest rates on cramped-down vehicle loans in Chapter 13 bankruptcy.\n\n**Interest Rate Determination:**\nBankruptcy courts often apply the Till v. SCS Credit Corp. (2004) \"prime-plus\" approach, adding a risk adjustment of one to three percent to the prime rate. The creditor is entitled to receive the prime interest rate plus 1 or 2 points, as set forth in Till v. SCS Credit Corp., 541 U.S. 465 (2004).\n\n**Relationship to 11 U.S.C. § 1325(a) and § 506:**\nThe Robinson court said that § 1325(a) as amended prohibited bifurcation, but it did not overrule Till v. SCS Credit Corp., which clearly allowed modification of interest rates by the court in a chapter 13. Given Congress's knowledge of the Till decision and Congress's decision not to change to the applicable statutory language when enacting BAPCPA, Till remains good law and is the proper interest rate at which secured claims must be paid to meet the requirements of § 1325(a)(5)(B)(ii). The interest rate that satisfies the \"present value\" requirement of cramdown is governed not by contract terms, but by Till.\n\nThe search results contain extensive discussion of how the Till rate applies to vehicle loan modifications in Chapter 13 cramdown cases, particularly regarding the interaction with § 1325(a) and § 506.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"e37243b1-8928-4bae-b05b-018129281775","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:27:51.820Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"2489b9d8-506c-421a-a795-3ffe4f79e2d7","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Consumer Finance\",\"url\":\"https://www.severson.com/consumer-finance/7th-circuit-rules-on-bapcpas-hanging-paragraph/\"},{\"title\":\"910 Cars in Chapter 13 - Philadelphia Bankruptcy Attorney\",\"url\":\"https://thephiladelphiabankruptcyattorney.com/2393/910-cars-in-chapter-13/\"},{\"title\":\"What Is The 910-Day Cramdown Rule? | A Bankruptcy Law Firm, LLC\",\"url\":\"https://bensonlawfirms.com/910-day-cramdown-rule/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the hanging paragraph, 910-day vehicle purchase prohibition, and Chapter 13 cramdown under BAPCPA:\n\n## The Hanging Paragraph & 910-Day Rule\n\nThe \"hanging paragraph\" is commonly referred to by this name because it was added to the end of Section 1325(a) of the Bankruptcy Code without a sub-section number. When the Bankruptcy Abuse Prevention and Consumer Protection Act (BAPCPA) revised the U.S. Bankruptcy Code in 2005, Section 1325 was amended to add this new paragraph.\n\n## What the Hanging Paragraph Prohibits\n\nThe hanging paragraph is generally understood as preventing a \"cram-down\" of an auto lender's secured claim at less than the full amount of the lender's claim — regardless of the car's true value (which is almost always less than the claim amount) — if the loan was made within 910 days of the debtor's filing of the case.\n\n## Requirements for the Prohibition to Apply\n\nThe 910-day rule only protects a lender if all three of the following conditions are met: the loan was taken out within the 910 days (about 2.5 years) before you filed for bankruptcy; the loan was a Purchase Money Security Interest (PMSI), meaning the money was used specifically to buy the vehicle; and the vehicle was acquired for your personal use (not for a business or as an investment).\n\n## Court Interpretation\n\nSince enactment, majority of courts interpreting the hanging paragraph hold that it precludes a Chapter 13 debtor from using § 506 to cram down a 910-day vehicle. When a creditor holds a valid purchase-money security interest in a vehicle purchased within the 910-day period immediately preceding the Chapter 13 petition filing, the debtor is required to pay the full amount of the creditor's claim over the life of their Chapter 13 plan, irrespective of the value of the vehicle.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"436a75f3-48e5-490c-95df-953b602252b2","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:28:08.459Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"787f39fc-327c-4f98-ad5a-e5c90f23a649","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Yarnall, 22 F.3d 190 (8th Cir. 1994), where debtors argued that the plain meaning of the § 1225(b) requirement obligated them to pay \"projected\" (predicted or estimated) disposable income, but the Eighth Circuit disagreed, saying a plain reading \"yields an absurd result\".\n\nThe second opinion is In re Broken Bow Ranch, Inc., 33 F.3d 1005 (8th Cir. 1994), where the Eighth Circuit declared that creditors may require a final disposable income determination at the end of the plan to ensure the debtor does not \"accumulate an unreasonably large reserve of funds that would provide a windfall at the time of discharge\".\n\n## Modification Standards Under § 1329\n\nWhen the debtor or a creditor objects to modification under § 1329, the trustee \"must bear the burden of showing a substantial change in the debtor's ability to pay since the confirmation hearing and that the prospect of the change had not already been taken into account at the time of confirmation\".\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"bb27c3e8-5255-43a9-8fd3-285aec115def","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:28:08.503Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"df16dcab-4e92-46ea-a09e-c0bf3cc3a65b","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Conversely, a surplus means an amount by which the current escrow account balance exceeds the target balance for the account.\n\n## Servicer Requirements\n\nFor each escrow account, the servicer shall conduct an escrow account analysis to determine whether a surplus, shortage or deficiency exists. The servicer must use the escrow account analysis to determine whether a surplus, shortage, or deficiency exists and must make any adjustments to the account.\n\n## Bankruptcy Exception\n\nIf at the time the servicer conducts the escrow account analysis the borrower is more than 30 days overdue, then the servicer is exempt from the requirements of submitting an annual escrow account statement to the borrower, and this applies in cases of default, foreclosure, or bankruptcy.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"37c279ec-a171-4771-b09a-a4bb7986e589","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:28:26.226Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"962c5c6a-ea5b-4559-88bf-a22e3d855996","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Here's what the search results show:\n\n## Case Overview\n\n*In re Gregory A. Lemaire*, 883 F.2d 1373 (8th Cir. 1989), addressed the dischargeability under Chapter 13 of the Bankruptcy Reform Act of a civil judgment owed to an assault victim by an assailant.\n\n## Good Faith Factors\n\nThe bankruptcy court determined that LeMaire's plan was proposed in good faith after properly considering all the factors set forth by the court in *Estus*. The Eighth Circuit outlined factors to be examined in order to properly apply a good-faith standard, including motivation and sincerity of the debtor, whether there were inaccuracies in the plan that would mislead the court, and the extent of preferential treatment.\n\nIn evaluating good faith, the bankruptcy court considered the principle of fresh start which is the cornerstone of bankruptcy law, noting that LeMaire, after serving his sentence, was getting back on his feet both professionally and financially, that the plan proposed to repay a significant portion of the judgment, and that LeMaire and his parents would make substantial sacrifices under the plan.\n\n## Key Holding\n\nThe court affirmed the bankruptcy court's finding of good faith, applying a totality of circumstances approach without mandating a minimum repayment, and concluded that the debtor met the Chapter 13 plan requirements.\n\nNote: The case was later vacated and reheard en banc, with the Eighth Circuit ultimately reversing the panel decision in 1990 (*Handeen v. LeMaire*, 898 F.2d 1346 (8th Cir. 1990)).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b034b17c-31d1-4c4d-90ca-9c122871e8a4","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:28:26.330Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"2c1a912d-2e1f-4abe-adb8-e9c5be348159","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Spouse Liability\n\nThe Act eliminates automatically transferring medical debt to a patient's spouse under Minn. Stat. 519.05. This means as of October 1, medical debt will no longer be automatically transferred to one's spouse.\n\n## Spouse Provisions\n\nSpouses will no longer be forced to pay off each other's medical debt in life or in death. More specifically, the protections include bans on automatically transferring medical debt to one's spouse upon their passing.\n\n## Retroactive Application\n\nAn important limitation is that the law isn't retroactive, so any debt accrued before Oct. 1 is not impacted.\n\n## Effective Dates\n\nOn October 1, 2024, significant pieces of the Minnesota Debt Fairness Act took effect. Additionally, on August 1, 2024, provisions of the bill improving bankruptcy protections took effect, and on April 1, 2025, new income-based limits on wage garnishment will take effect.\n\nFor the complete full text of the statute and all detailed provisions, you may want to consult the Minnesota Revisor of Statutes website directly or the official Attorney General's summary documents referenced in these sources.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b03db036-2460-4dea-98b0-121c8b69f0b3","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:28:39.870Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"800425fe-4a46-4b1a-8ad8-de1555030b5d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

and the extension beyond the original commitment period under 11 U.S.C. § 1329(c):\n\n## Key Provision\n\nA plan modified under this section may not provide for payments over a period that expires after the applicable commitment period under section 1325(b)(1)(B) after the time that the first payment under the original confirmed plan was due, unless the court, for cause, approves a longer period, but the court may not approve a period that expires after five years after such time.\n\n## Application\n\nThis provision establishes important limits on plan modification:\n\n1. **Default Limit**: A modified plan cannot extend beyond the applicable commitment period (which is typically 3 years for debtors below median income or 5 years for debtors above median income under § 1325(b)(1)(B)).\n\n2. **Extension \"For Cause\"**: § 1329(c) allows the court, for cause, to approve a longer period than the 36-month applicable commitment period to make those payments.\n\n3. **Absolute Maximum**: The court may not approve a period that expires after five years after such time.\n\nThis means that while courts have discretion to extend the plan period beyond the original commitment period when \"cause\" is demonstrated, they cannot extend it beyond five years from the date the first payment under the original confirmed plan was due.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the Here are the relevant findings:\n\n## Key Findings:\n\n**Post-Petition 401(k) Contributions and the Estate:**\n\nSection 1306 incorporates the exclusions from property of the estate found in Section 541(b), and post-petition contributions to an employer-sponsored 401(k) plan are not property of the Chapter 13 estate and therefore not included in projected disposable income.\n\n**General Definition of Property in Chapter 13:**\n\nSection 1306 broadens the definition of property of the estate for chapter 13 purposes to include all property acquired and all earnings from services performed by the debtor after the commencement of the case. More specifically, under chapter 13, the definition is augmented by §1306(a), which provides that property of the estate includes all property of the kind specified in §541 that the debtor acquires after the commencement of the case but before the case is closed, dismissed or converted, and earnings from services performed by the debtor after commencement.\n\n**The Estate Vesting Issue:**\n\nThere is significant case law addressing the tension between Section 1306(a) and Section 1327(b). Pursuant to section 1306(a) all property acquired by the debtor after confirmation becomes property of the estate, and once the debtor earns new income after confirmation, this after-acquired property becomes property of the estate.\n\nThe search results indicate this is a complex and disputed area of bankruptcy law with different courts adopting varying interpretations regarding when property vests in the debtor versus remaining part of the estate post-confirmation.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4d33ca6b-efa4-45df-8288-1ed0cb917296","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:29:08.197Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"2db50d3b-9507-461e-bd76-f3b9fbe0f635","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Ocwen (Bankruptcy Discharge Violation)\nAfter receiving a bankruptcy discharge, Ocwen immediately began trying to collect money from Saccameno that it was not owed and threatening foreclosure again. In this case, the court held that Ocwen's conduct was reprehensible, but not to an extreme degree. Judge St. Eve ruled that these factors pointed toward a substantial punitive damages award, but not one even approaching the $3,000,000 awarded here. The jury's $3 million punitive damage award was ultimately reduced on appeal to $582,000.\n\n### Beecroft v. Ocwen\nTracee A. Beecroft says that after she discharged a mortgage debt through bankruptcy, Ocwen called her cell phone 58 times, using an automated dialing system for at least some of those calls. Beecroft claimed the constant calls were so stressful that she suffered a miscarriage. Ocwen settled the action in June 2019 by agreeing pay $21.5 million in damages, plus about $4.7 million for the plaintiff's attorney fees.\n\n### Greer v. Nationstar (Mr. Cooper) - Credit Reporting Issue\nUnder Metro 2 reporting guidelines — the industry standard adopted by all three bureaus — servicers are expected to update a borrower's Consumer Information Indicator code upon discharge, clearing the bankruptcy flag and allowing normal payment history to report on the credit file. The lawsuit alleges Nationstar never made that update.\n\n### Nationstar Settlements\nThe proposed judgment and order, if entered by the court, would require Nationstar to pay approximately $73 million in redress to more than 40,000 harmed borrowers. It would also require Nationstar to pay a $1.5 million civil penalty to the Bureau.\n\nThese cases demonstrate significant liability exposure for mortgage servicers that fail to respect bankruptcy discharge orders or properly update credit reporting after discharge.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"059364cb-9203-4790-8127-20aea6084050","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:29:09.651Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"fca07cf3-86e1-4678-81ae-3a48cc1774fd","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}

Items of General Interest\",\"url\":\"https://www.irs.gov/pub/irs-drop/a-98-89.pdf\"},{\"title\":\"IRS VIOLATED AUTOMATIC STAY BUT VIOLATIONS WEREN'T WILLFUL. | Tax Notes\",\"url\":\"https://www.taxnotes.com/research/federal/court-documents/court-opinions-and-orders/irs-violated-automatic-stay-but-violations-werent-willful/1kvxw\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about IRC Section 7433, willful violations of bankruptcy automatic stay/discharge provisions, and case law on damages awards:\n\n## Overview of Section 7433\n\nSection 7433(e) of the Internal Revenue Code creates a cause of action for taxpayers who suffer damages when the IRS willfully violates the automatic stay or discharge provisions.\n\n## Definition of \"Willful\" Violation\n\nActions for damages caused by the violation of section 362 or 524 of the Bankruptcy Code are limited to willful violations. The case law indicates that willfulness requires intentional conduct. In one case, the IRS's actions were willful regarding a Notice of Levy and levy, even though one employee or one unit of the IRS did not know of the bankruptcy, because the IRS as an organization clearly knew of the bankruptcy filing.\n\nHowever, Section 7433(e) requires proof that a specific IRS officer or employee, rather than the Service as a whole, willfully violated the discharge order, and a creditor should not be held in contempt of an order where the violation was inadvertent and due to clerical error.\n\n## Damages Awards Framework\n\n**Limits on Damages:**\nThe maximum amount of damages that may be awarded for willful violations of section 362 or 524 of the Bankruptcy Code under subsection (e) is $1,000,000.\n\n**Types of Damages:**\nThe regulations define direct, economic damages as actual, pecuniary damages sustained by the taxpayer as a result of the willful violation of section 362 or 524 of the Bankruptcy Code. Injuries such as inconvenience, loss of reputation, and emotional distress are not compensable except to the extent they result in actual pecuniary loss.\n\n## Case Law Examples\n\n**Williams-McAuliffe Case:** A bankruptcy court held that the IRS's repeated collection notices and delays in abating a couple's discharged tax debts willfully violated the discharge, entitling the couple to damages under Section 7433, but the court limited the award to penalties and interest totaling less than $500 on a post-petition tax debt and declined to award other claimed damages or attorneys' fees.\n\n## Procedural Requirements\n\nNo petition for damages under section 7433(e) may be filed in a bankruptcy court unless the taxpayer first exhausts administrative remedies within the IRS.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5ab61d6f-ed90-4eb7-8e67-570357701acc","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:29:11.023Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"3cc58251-6139-49fe-b8b9-0a85b5788624","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}
