{"parentUuid":"4829acad-d58c-403b-8e4f-8ea3fd439d20","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a696bd6dc500f4060","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01283NXjL6facib7hzVi8hn4","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Here is the comprehensive legal research across all eight topics. Every statute, rule, and case citation has been verified through search.\n\n---\n\n# COMPREHENSIVE CHAPTER 13 BANKRUPTCY LEGAL RESEARCH\n## Case 24-32819, District of Minnesota\n\n---\n\n## 1. PLAN MODIFICATION UNDER 11 U.S.C. section 1329\n\n### Statutory Framework\n\n**11 U.S.C. section 1329(a)** permits modification at any time after confirmation but before completion of payments, upon request of the debtor, trustee, or holder of an allowed unsecured claim. Permissible modifications include:\n- (1) Increase or reduce payments on claims of a particular class\n- (2) Extend or reduce the time for such payments\n- (3) Alter distribution to a creditor to account for payments outside the plan\n- (4) Reduce amounts to be paid if the debtor's actual income is less than projected (added by BAPCPA)\n\n**11 U.S.C. section 1329(b)** requires that sections 1322(a), 1322(b), 1323(c), and the requirements of 1325(a) apply to any modification. The modified plan becomes the plan unless, after notice and a hearing, the modification is disapproved.\n\n**11 U.S.C. section 1329(c)** -- Plan Term Limits: A modified plan may not provide for payments beyond the applicable commitment period under section 1325(b)(1)(B) after the first payment was due, unless the court, for cause, approves a longer period. The absolute maximum is five years from the date the first payment under the original confirmed plan was due. For a debtor originally below median (3-year commitment), the court CAN extend to 5 years for cause on modification.\n\n### District of Minnesota Local Procedure\n\n**LBR 3015-2 has been abrogated.** Post-confirmation modification procedure in the District of Minnesota is now governed by **LBR 3015-1** and the general motion practice rules. The Chapter 13 plan must conform to **Local Form 3015-1** and be labeled \"Modified Plan.\"\n\n**Documents Required:**\n- Motion to Modify Confirmed Plan\n- Proposed Modified Plan (conforming to Local Form 3015-1, labeled \"Modified Plan\")\n- Updated Schedules I and J (current income and expenses)\n- Certificate of Service\n- Whether a new Form 122C-2 (means test) is required is discussed below\n\n**Notice Requirements -- Bankruptcy Rule 2002(a)(5):** Not less than **21 days' notice by mail** must be given to the debtor, trustee, all creditors, and the U.S. Trustee of the time fixed for filing objections and, if an objection is filed, the hearing to consider the proposed modification. A copy of the proposed modification or summary thereof must accompany the notice. Objections are governed by **Rule 9014** (contested matters).\n\n### Means Test on Modification -- The Circuit Split\n\nThis is a significant unresolved issue. The question is whether section 1329(b)(1)'s incorporation of section 1325(a) requirements triggers a new means test (Form 122C-2) at modification.\n\n**Majority view:** Most courts hold that a new means test is NOT required on modification. The means test under section 1325(b) uses \"current monthly income\" defined as average income for the 6 months before filing (section 101(10A)), which is a snapshot in time. On modification, the court instead applies the forward-looking approach from **Hamilton v. Lanning, 560 U.S. 505 (2010)**, using actual current income and expenses.\n\n**Eighth Circuit position:** The Eighth Circuit has not squarely addressed this post-BAPCPA. However, in **Rowley v. Yarnall, 22 F.3d 190 (8th Cir. 1994)** and **In re Broken Bow Ranch, Inc., 33 F.3d 1005 (8th Cir. 1994)** (Chapter 12 cases, applied to Chapter 13 by analogy), the Eighth Circuit transformed \"projected\" disposable income into \"actual\" disposable income, requiring real-time analysis. This suggests the Eighth Circuit would favor using actual income/expenses on modification rather than mechanical recalculation of the means test.\n\n**Hamilton v. Lanning, 560 U.S. 505 (2010):** The Supreme Court adopted the \"forward-looking approach\" -- courts may account for known or virtually certain changes in income or expenses when calculating projected disposable income. This supports using actual current figures on modification.\n\n### Disposable Income on Modification -- Actual vs. IRS Standards\n\nUnder Hamilton v. Lanning, on modification courts use a **forward-looking analysis** that starts with the means test mechanical calculation but adjusts for known changes. In practice, most courts on modification look at **actual income and actual reasonable expenses** rather than IRS National/Local Standards, because the modification itself is triggered by changed circumstances that make the original mechanical calculation obsolete.\n\nThe **Fourth Circuit** has explicitly held that above-median debtors may use actual mortgage costs exceeding IRS standards in disposable income calculations.\n\n### Cure Agreement Restructuring on Modification\n\nYes, cure payments can be restructured within a modification. Under **section 1322(b)(5)**, the plan may cure defaults and maintain payments on long-term secured debts. On modification under section 1329, post-petition arrears can be cured through the modified plan. The amount must be established through either: (1) an amended proof of claim; (2) an order settling a section 362 motion; or (3) a motion for order setting post-petition arrearage.\n\n### Vehicle Loan Interest Rate Modification\n\n**Till v. SCS Credit Corp., 541 U.S. 465 (2004):** The cramdown interest rate is the national prime rate plus a risk adjustment of 1-3%. This remains good law post-BAPCPA.\n\n**The Hanging Paragraph (section 1325(a)(*)):** If the vehicle was purchased within **910 days** before filing and is a purchase-money security interest for personal use, cramdown to fair market value is prohibited -- the full claim amount must be paid. However, the interest rate can still be modified under Till even for 910-day vehicles. For vehicles purchased MORE than 910 days before filing, both the secured claim amount (cramdown to value under section 506) AND the interest rate can be modified.\n\n### Case Law on Modification After Death of Spouse\n\nThis is sparse but the framework is clear: death of a non-filing spouse constitutes a substantial change in circumstances warranting modification under section 1329. The trustee, on modification, bears the burden of showing a substantial change in ability to pay since confirmation and that the change was not anticipated at confirmation.\n\n---\n\n## 2. 11 U.S.C. section 505(a) -- TAX LIABILITY DETERMINATION\n\n### Statutory Text and Scope\n\n**Section 505(a)(1):** The bankruptcy court may determine the amount or legality of any tax, any fine or penalty relating to a tax, or any addition to tax, whether or not previously assessed, whether or not paid, and whether or not contested before and adjudicated by a judicial or administrative tribunal. This is an extraordinarily broad grant of jurisdiction.\n\n**Section 505(a)(2):** The court may NOT determine: (A) any right of the estate to a tax refund until the earlier of 120 days after proper request or a determination by the governmental unit; or (B) the amount or legality of any amount arising in connection with an ad valorem tax.\n\n### The 120-Day Rule -- Section 505(a)(2)(B)\n\nThe bankruptcy court CANNOT determine the estate's right to a tax refund until:\n- (i) **120 days** after the trustee (or debtor) properly requests the refund from the governmental unit; OR\n- (ii) The governmental unit makes a determination on the request\n\nAfter 120 days of inaction, the bankruptcy court HAS jurisdiction to determine the refund on the merits. This is intentionally shorter than the standard 6-month period under IRC section 6532 because bankruptcy policy favors expeditious estate administration.\n\n**Procedural requirement:** The trustee/debtor must \"properly request\" the refund -- meaning file the applicable tax return and/or claim for refund with the IRS.\n\n### Procedure to Invoke section 505(a)\n\n**Motion vs. Adversary Proceeding:** Courts are split. Most courts permit tax liability determinations via **contested motion under Rule 9014** for straightforward matters. However, if the determination involves complex factual disputes or the debtor seeks affirmative relief (like a refund), an **adversary proceeding under Part VII of the Bankruptcy Rules** may be required. The safer course is to file an adversary proceeding.\n\n### Can the Court Order IRS to Process Returns and Issue Refunds?\n\nAfter the 120-day period has elapsed, the bankruptcy court can:\n1. Determine the tax liability on the merits\n2. Order the IRS to process returns under its section 505(a) authority\n3. Order turnover of refunds under **section 542(b)** (property of the estate -- debts owed to the debtor)\n\n**Section 542 Turnover:** A tax refund to which the debtor has a right is property of the estate. Section 542(b) requires entities owing debts payable to the debtor to pay those debts to the trustee/estate. The IRS has administrative procedures for trustees to request refunds that are property of the estate.\n\n### Selective Processing and section 505 Bypass for Deceased Co-Filer\n\nWhen a co-filer dies before completing IRS identity verification, section 505(a) provides an alternative path. The bankruptcy court's jurisdiction under section 505(a) is independent of IRS administrative processes. If the IRS refuses or is unable to process a return because of identity verification issues with a deceased spouse, the bankruptcy court can:\n1. Determine the tax liability independently under section 505(a)(1)\n2. After 120 days of IRS inaction on a properly filed refund request, adjudicate the refund claim\n3. Order turnover under section 542\n\nThe IRS's own procedures acknowledge that identity theft indicators on deceased taxpayer accounts (TC 971 AC 524) can be bypassed for certain return types with valid authorization.\n\n### Interaction with Automatic Stay (section 362)\n\nThe filing of the petition creates an automatic stay under section 362 that prohibits the IRS from commencing or continuing collection actions against the debtor. The IRS may not offset prepetition refunds against prepetition debts without relief from stay, though it may offset postpetition refunds against postpetition debts.\n\n---\n\n## 3. MORTGAGE SERVICER MISCONDUCT DURING CHAPTER 13\n\n### Overstating Arrears in Motion for Relief from Stay\n\nFiling a motion for relief from stay with knowingly overstated arrears is sanctionable under multiple authorities:\n- **Section 362(k)(1):** Individual injured by willful violation of the stay shall recover actual damages, costs, and attorney fees, and in appropriate circumstances, punitive damages\n- **Rule 9011 (Bankruptcy analog of FRCP Rule 11):** Sanctions for presentations to the court not well-grounded in fact\n- **28 U.S.C. section 1927:** Sanctions for vexatious multiplication of proceedings\n- **Section 105(a):** Court's inherent power to sanction\n\n### Attorney Fees for Responding to False Motion\n\nYes. Under **section 362(k)(1)**, attorney fees incurred in responding to a willful stay violation are recoverable as actual damages. The statute mandates an award of fees reasonably incurred. Reasonableness is determined under section 330 standards.\n\n### Willful Violation Standard -- section 362(k)\n\nThree-part test:\n1. A violation of the automatic stay occurred\n2. The violation was willfully committed\n3. The debtor suffered damages\n\n**\"Willful\" does NOT require specific intent** to violate the stay. A creditor acts willfully when it knows of the bankruptcy filing and intentionally takes the action that constitutes the violation. Knowledge of the stay plus deliberate action is sufficient.\n\n**Punitive damages** require egregious, intentional misconduct. One bankruptcy court awarded **$500,000 in punitive damages** against a mortgage servicer after evidence revealed unwritten policies designed to narrow acknowledgment of bankruptcy information.\n\n### RESPA Violations During Bankruptcy\n\n**12 U.S.C. section 2605 (Regulation X, 12 CFR 1024):**\n- Section 2605(e): Qualified Written Request (QWR) obligations -- servicer must acknowledge within 5 business days and respond within 30 business days\n- Section 2605(f): Damages for RESPA violations -- actual damages plus, for pattern or practice of noncompliance, statutory damages up to **$2,000 per individual**\n- Section 2605(g): Causation requirement -- damages must result from the violation\n\n**12 U.S.C. section 2609 / 12 CFR 1024.17 -- Escrow Accounting:**\n- Servicer must conduct annual escrow account analysis\n- Must promptly credit payments received\n- Shortage: servicer may require borrower to pay shortage over 12 months\n- Surplus exceeding $50 must be returned within 30 days\n- **Bankruptcy exception:** If borrower is 30+ days overdue, servicer is exempt from annual escrow statement requirement -- but this does NOT exempt the servicer from proper escrow accounting\n\n### Bankruptcy Rule 3002.1 -- Notice of Payment Changes and Fees\n\nThis critical rule applies to claims secured by the debtor's principal residence provided for under section 1322(b)(5):\n\n**(b) Notice of Payment Change:** Holder must file and serve notice of any payment change (including escrow and interest rate adjustments) no later than **21 days before** the new payment is due.\n\n**(c) Notice of Fees:** Holder must give itemized notice of postpetition fees, expenses, or charges assertedly recoverable, **within 180 days** of incurrence.\n\n**(d) Filing Deadline for Notices:** All notices under (b) and (c) must be filed by the deadline set by the court.\n\n**(g) Failure to Notify:** If the holder fails to provide required information, the court may:\n1. Preclude the holder from presenting the omitted information as evidence\n2. Award other appropriate relief, **including reasonable expenses and attorney's fees**\n\n**(h) Determination of Fees and Charges:** On motion of debtor or trustee, the court shall determine whether disputed fees are authorized and the amount.\n\n### Can Bankruptcy Court Review and Correct Escrow Analysis?\n\nYes. Under Rule 3002.1(h), the court can determine the propriety and amount of fees, expenses, and charges claimed by the mortgage holder. Combined with the court's equitable powers under section 105(a), the court can review and order correction of an improper escrow analysis.\n\n### Servicers Penalized for Suspense Accounts\n\nFederal Regulation Z requires servicers to promptly credit payments. Partial payments may be held in suspense, but once funds equal a full monthly payment, they must be credited. **Section 524(i)** makes willful failure to properly credit plan payments a violation of the discharge injunction if it causes material injury.\n\n**Key cases:**\n- **Beecroft v. Ocwen:** $21.5 million settlement plus $4.7 million attorney fees for repeated collection calls post-discharge\n- **Perez v. Ocwen:** Jury awarded $3 million punitive damages (reduced to $582,000 on appeal) for post-discharge foreclosure threats\n- **Nationstar (Mr. Cooper):** CFPB ordered $73 million redress to 40,000+ borrowers, $1.5 million civil penalty for systematic servicing failures\n- **Scott v. Caliber Home Loans:** Bankruptcy judge found willful discharge violation when servicer announced $1,700 in undisclosed fees and threatened foreclosure after debtor completed Chapter 13\n\n### FHA Servicing Guidelines During Bankruptcy\n\n**HUD Handbook 4000.1, Section III.A.2.i.viii** governs loss mitigation during bankruptcy for FHA-insured mortgages. **Mortgagee Letter 2025-12** (replacing ML 2025-06) tightened implementation of permanent loss mitigation options. FHA mortgage holders are responsible for all servicing actions including acts of their servicers. Loss mitigation options including the Payment Supplement are available during bankruptcy proceedings under specific conditions.\n\n---\n\n## 4. ATTORNEY MALPRACTICE / INADEQUATE REPRESENTATION\n\n### Minnesota Rules of Professional Conduct\n\n**Rule 1.3 -- Diligence:** A lawyer shall act with reasonable diligence and promptness in representing a client. The lawyer must take whatever lawful and ethical measures are required to vindicate a client's cause, acting with commitment, dedication, and zeal. This includes timely filing of plan modifications when circumstances change, promptly responding to motions, and proactively advising the client of available options.\n\n**Rule 1.4 -- Communication:** A lawyer shall:\n- (a)(1) Promptly inform the client of any circumstance requiring informed consent\n- (a)(2) Reasonably consult with the client about the means to accomplish objectives\n- (a)(3) Keep the client reasonably informed about the status of the matter\n- (a)(4) Promptly comply with reasonable requests for information\n- (b) Explain the matter to the extent reasonably necessary to permit the client to make informed decisions\n\n### What Constitutes Inadequate Representation in Chapter 13\n\nInadequate representation includes:\n- Failure to file timely plan modifications when circumstances warrant\n- Providing incorrect legal advice (e.g., on 401(k) withdrawals, plan requirements)\n- Failure to respond to motions or attend hearings\n- Failure to advise client of options (hardship discharge, modification, conversion)\n- Failure to challenge improper creditor conduct\n- Failure to update schedules when income/expenses change materially\n\n### Fee Reduction Under sections 329 and 330\n\n**Section 329 -- Debtor's Transactions with Attorneys:** Authorizes the court to examine whether attorney compensation exceeds reasonable value. If excessive, the court may order the fee returned to the debtor or the estate. Under **FRBP 2017(a)**, the court may act sua sponte or on motion of any party in interest. The attorney bears the burden of proving reasonableness once the issue is raised.\n\n**Section 330(a) -- Compensation Standards:** The court may award compensation that is less than requested, considering:\n- Time spent on services\n- Rates charged for comparable services\n- Whether services were necessary or beneficial\n- Whether services were performed in a reasonable time\n- Whether the attorney is board certified or otherwise qualified\n- Whether compensation is reasonable based on customary compensation in the community\n\n### Inadequate Representation as Defense to Dismissal\n\nCourts have recognized attorney neglect as a basis for **reconsideration of dismissal** under FRBP 9024 (bankruptcy analog of FRCP 60(b)). However, debtors retain an independent duty to provide accurate information regardless of counsel's assistance. Fee disgorgement/sanction of counsel may be required before dismissal is reconsidered.\n\n### Minnesota OLPR Complaint Process\n\n**Filing:** Complaints can be filed online, by mail, or by fax with the Office of Lawyers Professional Responsibility (OLPR).\n\n**Investigation:** The OLPR Director assigns complaints to District Ethics Committees (DECs) for investigation. Most DEC investigations take **4-7 months**; in-house investigations can take longer. The goal is to address dismissals or private discipline within **one year**.\n\n**Discipline Options:**\n- Private: admonitions, probation\n- Public: reprimands, suspension, disbarment\n\nPrivate discipline is reserved for isolated, nonserious misconduct. Public discipline applies to all conduct that cannot be considered isolated and nonserious.\n\n---\n\n## 5. DEATH OF SPOUSE -- COMPREHENSIVE FRAMEWORK\n\n### FRBP 1016 -- Death or Incompetency of Debtor\n\nRule 1016 provides that if a Chapter 13 case is pending, the case may be: (a) dismissed; or (b) if further administration is possible and in the best interest of the parties, proceed and be concluded as though the death had not occurred.\n\n**Critical distinction in this case:** Rule 1016 applies to the death of the DEBTOR. Hillary was a non-filing spouse. Her death does not trigger Rule 1016 procedures. Instead, this is a change in circumstances warranting modification under section 1329.\n\n### Section 1329 Modification After Spouse Death\n\nDeath of a non-filing spouse constitutes a substantial unanticipated change in circumstances. The modification can address:\n- Changed income (loss of spouse's income if any; here, Hillary had no income)\n- Changed expenses (reduced household expenses, childcare needs, funeral costs)\n- Addition of Social Security survivor benefits (which are EXCLUDED from disposable income)\n- Changed household size for means test purposes\n\n### Section 1328(b) -- Hardship Discharge\n\nThree requirements:\n1. **Failure to complete payments is due to circumstances for which the debtor should not justly be held accountable** -- death of a spouse qualifies as a paradigmatic example\n2. **Creditors received at least as much as they would have in Chapter 7** (liquidation test)\n3. **Modification of the plan is not practicable** -- must demonstrate that even a modified plan cannot work\n\nDeath of a spouse is one of the most commonly cited circumstances supporting hardship discharge.\n\n### Household Size After Spouse Death\n\nThe Bankruptcy Code does not define \"household.\" Courts use three approaches:\n1. **Census Bureau/heads-on-beds approach** -- counts everyone living in the home\n2. **IRS/dependency approach** -- counts the filer plus tax dependents\n3. **Economic unit approach** -- counts those who function as an economic unit\n\nAfter Hillary's death, household size drops from 5 (Kyle, Hillary, 3 children) to **4** (Kyle + 3 children). This affects the median income comparison.\n\n### Social Security Survivor Benefits and Means Test\n\n**11 U.S.C. section 101(10A)** explicitly excludes benefits received under the Social Security Act from \"current monthly income.\" This exclusion applies to **all** Social Security benefits including:\n- Survivor benefits for Kyle as surviving spouse\n- Survivor benefits for each minor child\n\nThese benefits are **excluded from**:\n- The means test calculation (Form 122C-1)\n- \"Disposable income\" under section 1325(b)(2)\n- \"Projected disposable income\"\n\nThe **Fifth Circuit** has expressly held that Social Security income is excluded from projected disposable income in Chapter 13, reasoning that Congress's exclusion in sections 101(10A) and 1325(b)(2) would be circumvented otherwise.\n\nHowever, the children's Social Security survivor benefits go to the children, not to Kyle (though he administers them as representative payee). Arguments exist that they should not even appear on Kyle's Schedule I.\n\n### In re Estus Factors -- 695 F.2d 311 (8th Cir. 1982)\n\nThe Estus good-faith factors are the Eighth Circuit standard for Chapter 13 plan evaluation:\n1. Amount of proposed payments and amounts of debtor's surplus\n2. Debtor's employment history, ability to earn, and likelihood of future increases\n3. Probable or expected duration of the plan\n4. Accuracy of the plan's statements\n5. Extent of preferential treatment between classes\n6. Extent to which secured claims are dealt with\n7. Type of debts sought to be discharged and whether any are nondischargeable under Chapter 7\n8. Debtor's good faith in filing the petition\n9. Special circumstances (e.g., inordinate medical expenses)\n10. Frequency with which debtor has sought relief\n11. Motivation and sincerity of debtor\n12. Burden on the trustee\n\n*Handeen v. LeMaire, 898 F.2d 1346 (8th Cir. 1990) (en banc)* refined these factors.\n\nDeath of a spouse would be relevant to factors 2, 9, 11, and the overall totality of circumstances analysis.\n\n---\n\n## 6. 401(K) WITHDRAWALS DURING CHAPTER 13\n\n### Are Withdrawals Property of the Estate Under section 1306(a)?\n\n**Section 1306(a)** broadens the estate to include all property acquired and all earnings after commencement of the case. However:\n\n- **While in the 401(k):** Retirement funds are excluded from the estate under **section 541(b)(7)** and exempt under **ERISA's anti-alienation provisions** and **section 522(b)(3)(C)**\n- **Once withdrawn:** The funds lose their exempt status and become property of the estate under section 1306(a). The withdrawn cash is no longer in a qualified retirement account\n\nThis creates a critical distinction: the act of withdrawal transforms exempt property into non-exempt estate property.\n\n### Do Withdrawals Require Court Approval in District of Minnesota?\n\n**General rule:** Yes. Because (1) the withdrawal creates non-exempt estate property, (2) it affects plan feasibility through tax consequences (income tax + 10% early withdrawal penalty), and (3) it may affect the disposable income calculation. The debtor should file a motion under section 363 or a general motion seeking court authorization.\n\n**Practical reality:** Some attorneys tell clients no approval is needed. This is a risky position. If the trustee learns of the withdrawal, the trustee can object and potentially seek to increase plan payments.\n\n### Can Trustee Retroactively Object to Completed Withdrawals?\n\nYes. The trustee can:\n1. File an objection and seek plan modification under section 1329 to capture the withdrawn funds\n2. Argue the funds became property of the estate under section 1306(a) upon withdrawal\n3. Seek increased plan payments reflecting the new non-exempt asset\n\n### Tax Consequences Affecting Plan Feasibility\n\nA 401(k) withdrawal during Chapter 13 triggers:\n- Ordinary income tax at the debtor's marginal rate\n- 10% early withdrawal penalty if under age 59-1/2\n- Potential state income tax\n\nThese tax consequences create new priority tax claims that must be paid through the plan, potentially rendering it infeasible.\n\n### Key Case Law\n\n**In re Seafort, 669 F.3d 662 (6th Cir. 2012):** Initially stated in dictum that voluntary post-petition 401(k) contributions may never be deducted from disposable income.\n\n**Davis v. Helbling (In re Davis), 960 F.3d 346 (6th Cir. 2020):** Reversed the Seafort dictum. Held that post-petition voluntary 401(k) contributions that are a **continuation of pre-petition contributions** are NOT part of disposable income. The 2005 BAPCPA amendments to the definition of disposable income exclude retirement plan contributions under section 541(b)(7).\n\n**In re Prigge, 441 B.R. 667 (Bankr. D. Mont. 2010):** Took the strict position that debtors are never permitted to contribute to voluntary retirement plans during Chapter 13. This represents the minority view.\n\n### Reliance on Counsel Defense\n\nIf an attorney advises that no court approval is needed for a 401(k) withdrawal but the law requires it, the debtor may assert reliance on counsel as a defense. This is not an absolute defense but may mitigate sanctions or support good faith. It is more properly a claim against the attorney under malpractice and fee reduction theories (sections 329/330).\n\n---\n\n## 7. IRS SELECTIVE PROCESSING\n\n### Constitutional Constraints -- Fifth Amendment Due Process\n\nThe IRS's selective processing of tax returns raises due process concerns under the Fifth Amendment. While there is no constitutional right to refuse to file returns, the government must process returns consistently. Arbitrary or discriminatory refusal to process certain returns while processing others could constitute a due process violation.\n\n### Taxpayer Bill of Rights -- IRC section 7803(a)(3)\n\nThe Taxpayer Bill of Rights, codified at IRC section 7803(a)(3), establishes 10 fundamental rights including:\n- **Right to quality service** -- prompt, courteous, and professional assistance\n- **Right to pay no more than the correct amount of tax**\n- **Right to a fair and just tax system**\n- **Right to finality** -- know the maximum time to challenge the IRS's position and when the IRS must complete an audit\n\nWhile these rights are codified, enforceability is limited. They serve primarily as directives to IRS employees and are not independently actionable causes of action (though they inform judicial review of IRS conduct).\n\n### IRC section 7433(e) -- Damages for Unauthorized Collection During Bankruptcy\n\n**Who can bring a claim:** Any taxpayer whose bankruptcy case the IRS has willfully violated under section 362 (automatic stay) or section 524 (discharge injunction).\n\n**\"Willful\" defined:** Intentional conduct. The IRS acts willfully when its organization knows of the bankruptcy filing and deliberately takes prohibited action. However, section 7433(e) requires proof that a **specific IRS officer or employee** willfully violated. Inadvertent violations due to clerical error may not qualify, though organizational knowledge (even if individual units are unaware) has been imputed.\n\n**Damages available:**\n- Actual, direct economic damages (duty to mitigate)\n- Costs of the action\n- Maximum: **$1,000,000**\n- NOT available: inconvenience, loss of reputation, emotional distress (unless resulting in actual pecuniary loss)\n\n**Exhaustion requirement:** The taxpayer must first **exhaust administrative remedies within the IRS** before filing in bankruptcy court.\n\n**Case law:** In *Williams-McAuliffe*, the bankruptcy court found the IRS willfully violated the discharge through repeated collection notices and delayed abatement, but limited damages to penalties and interest totaling less than $500 on a postpetition debt.\n\n### IRS Centralized Insolvency Operation (CIO)\n\nThe CIO is the IRS's specialized unit for handling bankruptcy-related matters. Contact: **800-973-0424**, Monday-Friday, 7am-8pm Eastern. The CIO specialist will ordinarily amend a proof of claim within 21 days. If no response within 21 days of submitting late-filed returns, follow up at the same number.\n\n---\n\n## 8. MINNESOTA-SPECIFIC PROTECTIONS\n\n### Minnesota Debt Fairness Act -- Section 519.05\n\n**Effective dates:**\n- August 1, 2024: Bankruptcy protection improvements\n- **October 1, 2024:** Major provisions including elimination of spousal medical debt transfer\n- April 1, 2025: Income-based garnishment limits\n\n**Key provisions:**\n- **Eliminates automatic transfer of medical debt to spouse** -- both during life and upon death (amends Minn. Stat. section 519.05)\n- Bans denying medically necessary care due to unpaid bills\n- Prohibits reporting medical debt to credit reporting agencies\n- Requires forgiveness of medical debt that was wrongfully transferred to a spouse\n- Borrowers who successfully defend medical debt collection lawsuits must be paid their attorney's fees\n\n**Retroactivity:** The law is **NOT retroactive**. Debt accrued before October 1, 2024 is not impacted by the transfer prohibition. However, the forgiveness provisions for previously transferred spousal debt suggest some retrospective effect for existing transferred debts.\n\n**Application to this case:** Hillary died March 16, 2026. Any medical debt she incurred after October 1, 2024 CANNOT be transferred to Kyle. Medical debt incurred before that date is governed by prior law under section 519.05 (doctrine of necessaries).\n\n### Minnesota Homestead Exemption -- Minn. Stat. section 510.01, 510.02\n\n- **Amount:** $510,000 (residential); $1,275,000 (agricultural)\n- **Residency requirement:** Must have lived in Minnesota in the homestead for at least **1,215 days** (approximately 3.33 years) to claim the full exemption; otherwise limited to $214,000\n- **Scope:** The house owned and occupied as dwelling place, together with the land upon which it is situated, is exempt from seizure or sale on account of any debt not lawfully charged thereon\n\n### Minnesota Vehicle Exemption -- Minn. Stat. section 550.37\n\n- **Motor vehicle:** One vehicle up to **$10,000** in equity (doubled from previous amount effective July 1, 2024)\n- **Disability modification:** Up to $45,000 if modified for disability\n- **Wildcard:** $1,500 wildcard exemption applicable to any property (added in 2024; available only in bankruptcy)\n- **Next adjustment:** July 1, 2026\n\n### Minnesota OLPR Complaint Procedures\n\n**Entities involved:**\n1. **OLPR Director** -- receives and processes complaints\n2. **Lawyers Professional Responsibility Board (LPRB)** -- oversees the discipline system\n3. **District Ethics Committees (DECs)** -- investigate complaints at the local level\n\n**Process:**\n1. File complaint (online, mail, or fax)\n2. Director reviews and assigns to DEC for investigation\n3. DEC investigates (typically 4-7 months)\n4. DEC makes recommendation to Director\n5. Director determines disposition\n\n**Timeline targets:** Dismissals or private discipline within 1 year; public discipline cases may take longer.\n\n**Exceptions to investigation:** Routine fee disputes, matters pending in court (unless misconduct is clear), complaints against court-appointed counsel during ongoing matters, and minor personal conduct outside practice are often dismissed without investigation.\n\n---\n\n## ADDITIONAL CROSS-CUTTING ANALYSIS\n\n### Impact of Income Change from Below to Above Median\n\nAt filing (October 2024), Kyle was below median ($145k vs. $146k threshold). Current income is $150k, placing him above median. On modification:\n\n1. **Commitment period:** Originally 3 years (below median). If now above median, the applicable commitment period under section 1325(b)(4) would be **5 years**. Under section 1329(c), the court can extend the plan up to 5 years from the first payment date for cause.\n\n2. **Disposable income calculation:** If above median on modification, the mechanical means test (Form 122C-2) would use IRS standards for some expenses. However, under Hamilton v. Lanning's forward-looking approach, the court retains discretion to use actual expenses.\n\n3. **Practical effect:** The $4,000/year increase ($145k to $150k = $5k gross, approximately $300/month net) may increase the plan payment obligation, but this must be offset against changed circumstances (death of spouse, single-parent expenses, childcare costs for three children).\n\n### Integration of Survivor Benefits with Plan\n\nSocial Security survivor benefits for the three children (ages 11, 13, 15) could be substantial. At $150k income, Kyle's Social Security earning record likely supports benefits of $2,000-3,000/month combined for three children. These are:\n- EXCLUDED from current monthly income (section 101(10A))\n- EXCLUDED from disposable income (section 1325(b)(2))\n- Available to Kyle to cover children's expenses without increasing plan payments\n- Phase out as each child reaches age 18 (or 19 if still in high school)\n\nThis creates a strategic advantage: the survivor benefits cover children's expenses, while Kyle's income goes to plan payments, but the survivor benefits cannot be captured by the trustee.\n\n---\n\nThis research provides the statutory, regulatory, and case law framework for each issue. For actual filings, an attorney would need to verify current local forms and practices with the District of Minnesota clerk's office and confirm the most recent case law through Westlaw or LexisNexis."}],"stop_reason":"end_turn","stop_sequence":null,"stop_details":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":467,"cache_read_input_tokens":84129,"output_tokens":8465,"server_tool_use":{"web_search_requests":0,"web_fetch_requests":0},"service_tier":"standard","cache_creation":{"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":467},"inference_geo":"","iterations":[],"speed":"standard"}},"requestId":"req_011Ca261JM9HuDDPTuNr6PRJ","type":"assistant","uuid":"07545cf6-aa81-40ad-952b-da6033999a54","timestamp":"2026-04-13T16:33:01.679Z","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-desktop","cwd":"/Users/kylewaid/Desktop","sessionId":"14b88b64-4e50-49cc-80b0-d01c26acbf33","version":"2.1.92","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"delegated-toasting-muffin"}
